Some may feel that it justifies the entire book that's come before, others will feel that it negates it.  I fall somewhere in the middle.  I can tell you that I finished the novel with a smile on my face, amazed (and oddly charmed) by McEwan's ability to pull the rug out from under his readers.  \\"How clever, unexpected and satisfying,\\" I thought.  Then I felt mildly manipulated, even cheated somewhat.  Imagine eating a rich, dense piece of chocolate cake (indulging one's sweet tooth, if you will), delighting in its decadence and sinfulness -- only to find out it's actually low-fat and gluten-free.  You've still enjoyed it, but now realize how little actual substance there was to the darn thing.  That's my experience of \\"Sweet Tooth.\\"  I enjoyed it tremendously, was never bored...but expected more.  It's a clever exercise tricked out as literature.  But then \\"tricking\\" is what espionage is all about, isn't it: things aren't exactly what they seem.<br /><br />McEwan is a master of misdirection, so let's give him full credit where it's due; he has obviously written the book he wants.  Readers drawn by this sweet's tempting political and sociological wrappings, however, are likely to be disappointed by the rather bland, banal treat within.

She writes as if you are reading a fairy tale, but a delicious, mysterious fairy tale bound to leave you melancholy with what if's and of possibilities lost and found.<br /><br />Reading a Kate Morton novel gives you a new appreciation for detective work and as she throws you the tiny crumbs that will eventually lead to the final reveal, you the reader play mini-detective and try to piece all of the parts together, discarding possibilities, altering theories and expanding on ideas.<br />I had a few theories that changed as I read it but surprisingly I suspected the final big reveal, although there were a few loose ends that I needed filling in. Regardless, it took nothing away from my enjoyment; I loved every heart-pounding moment.<br /><br />The characters, as always, are sympathetic yet humanized, coming to life with every flip of the page. I particularly loved Laurel and Jimmy. Laurel especially gives voice to the doubts and theories that ran through my head. Jimmy is the quintessential good guy, you can't help but love him.<br /><br />Kate Morton does with aplomb what would be far-fetched and unbelievable with other authors. Her tales are full of mystery and twists and turns and she can leave you guessing until the final reveal. The Secret Keeper is available Today, October 16. Grab your copy, you won't be disappointed.

1451627297|5.0|A combination of curiosity, and a watershed moment led Jake Epping through a rabbit hole and into 1958 in the gripping new novel by Stephen King.<br /><br />Jake Epping is a high school English and GED teacher. When he asks students to write about an event that changed their lives, he never thought it would change his. Jake's watershed moment occurs when he reads Henry Dunning's essay about the night his father came home and killed his mother, his sister and his brothers with a sledgehammer.<br /><br />Two years later Jake is summoned to a diner owned by his friend Al Templeton. Al, who looks as if he has aged 20 years overnight, explains the secrets of the diner's pantry to Jake. It is a portal into a particular day in 1958. You always go to the same date and time and no matter how long you are gone, only two minutes has elapsed in the present time. You will still age though, so if you spend two years in the past you will age two years (hence Al's appearance). Al is unable to continue what he has made his life's mission so he recruits Jake for the job - to go back to 1958 and stop Lee Harvey Oswald from firing the fatal shots that changed our world forever.<br /><br />I thought this book was going to be about a man trying to prevent John F. Kennedy's assassination and what the world would be like if he succeeded. I was wrong. It was so much more. I loved every page of this book, from the first line. While the meat of the novel is Jake's mission, the heart of the novel is the love story. It's about a time when life was simpler, people were more trusting, the air smells better and the food tastes better.<br /><br />Jake and Sadie are two phenomenal characters, both strong and well-written. They will go down in my own personal hall of fame. The supporting cast is just as good and the time period becomes a character in itself. It is clear how well researched this novel was. The history of that time, of Lee Harvey Oswald and JFK is fascinating.<br /><br />11/22/63 has been hailed as King's Tour de Force.  King does a phenomenal job of bringing the late 50's and early 60's to life. I also think it is his love letter to that era. I could envision myself in a full skirted dress doing the Lindy Hop and the Madison right along with Jake and Sadie. And isn't that a mark of a great writer, to be so engrossed in the story we feel we are right there with the characters?<br /><br />While I would have preferred a different ending, I know it is the only reasonable way this novel could have ended. But if time is elastic and different threads are being pulled at all times, maybe somewhere, somehow things are different, simpler. If you haven't read this novel, do it. It was one of the best books I have read in a long time.<br /><br />Read the alternate ending here and ball your eyes out all over again, I did.

Day only begins peeling their layers and leaves the reader with the heart pounding expectation of more. Eva is strong willed and determined. Although she is battling issues from her past, she is coping and she knows what she wants in the future. He also respects Eva and her issues and he is willing to give her what she needs.<br /><br /> Eva is a recent graduate and she has moved to New York with her best friend Cary Taylor to start her career at an advertising agency. She meets Gideon in a chance encounter at the Crossfire building, the building they both work.<br /><br />Gideon begins his pursuit of Eva but makes it clear he doesn't have any inclination for anything more than a sexual relationship. Eva naturally balks, she is open to a friends with benefits arrangement but she needs more, such as knowing and respecting her partner. As Eva keeps denying Gideon, he wants her more and agrees to her demands. They begin a relationship that has both of them wanting more from the other and as their relationship progresses, Eva is forced to confront the demons of her past while knowing Gideon is hiding demons of his own.<br /><br />This is the first Sylvia Day book I've read and I will definitely check out her other works. She is a phenomenal writer. She gave the novel two incredibly complex and screwed up characters and she made them believable and lovable. Her writing style is fast-paced and she gives attention to the details.<br /><br />Yes, there is sex, lots of smokin' hot sex but that's not all this book is about. There are dinners and romance and there are the deeper emotional issues that Day zones in on which give the characters the intensity that is the heart of Bared to You.<br /><br />My final thoughts are I loved this book. I usually stay within the bounds of contemporary or regency romance and I'm so glad I stepped out and read this. I loved every minute of it and Reflected in You (the second book in the trilogy, due out in October) has now made its way to the top of my can't wait for list.

0375507256|5.0|This book starts off difficultly - know that going into it. The first section plops you down into the middle of a journal entry. The style of writing is old-fashioned and difficult for our modern tastes, and there are characters and events that are referenced from previous pages in the journal that we never got to read. Because of the style of writing in this and the sixth section, I'd say this novel is not for the light reader, or those who only stick with \\"airport\\" or \\"summer reading\\" type novels. But for people who actually like to think about meanings beyond simple events, this book will not disappoint.<br /><br />As already mentioned the initial style of the book is fairly difficult (Melville-esqe according to the author), but is completely different from the style of the second section, which is again completely different from the style of the third section, and so on through each of the different parts of the book. By the second section I was incredibly impressed that the same author had written such dissimilar styles, and by the end of the book I was pretty much blown away.<br /><br />As far as the actual story itself goes, it is really quite complicated, and you are not ever going to get concrete answers to some of your questions. For ease of reading, I actually followed along two different websites while I was reading this book: one was basically giving me footnotes about some of the references (and there are many) in the book which I may or may not be aware of. The other website I used was a sort of on-line book club, which let me read other people's thoughts/perceptions of each section of this book. And I am glad I did, because there is so much contained here that I would never have picked it all up on my own. Some of the most striking parallels you probably won't catch until subsequent read-throughs, and this book is the first book in a long time that I considered re-reading the moment I finished it.<br /><br />If you're looking into this book because of the movie, don't expect it to be the same. The novel flirts with the reincarnation idea (one character believes this to be true of herself), but never states that this is the case. In fact, in some ways it actively negates this idea (aside from one character denouncing reincarnation as \\"silly\\", it's actually physically impossible time-line-wise for two of the characters (or those around them) to be reincarnated versions from the previous section). If you are expecting a Tom Hanks/Halle Berry type of romance, forget it. The book doesn't have a single romantic plot within it (note: there are characters who are in love with other characters, but it is never pivotal to the main point of any section). If, instead, you are hoping for a book which will provoke thought and keep you entertained along the way, you could not go wrong with Cloud Atlas.

1451620829|5.0|Want to know how much Peter was being paid per show on the Reunion Tour? Want to know about Ace's big lie to Peter that permanently damaged their friendship? Or maybe you want to play a game and count how many times Peter mentions \\"Gene\\" and \\"herpes\\" in the same sentence throughout the book. Surprisingly, this is a pretty raw and honest account of Peter's life and career, and is definitely the best KISS bio to date. Peter spares no one, including himself. If you're a long-time KISS fan, you'll find that the venom he has for Paul and Gene runs pretty deep, but you can also definitely see why they referred to Peter as the \\"complainer.\\" But Peter also holds himself over the fire - you learn how he blew through all of his KISS money (then AND now), destroyed his marriages, got lost in drug and alcohol abuse in and out of KISS, was suicidal many times, etc. And even post-KISS, you learn how much the failure of his solo album a few years ago almost did him in.<br /><br />Peter really sticks it to Gene, Paul, Doc McGee, Tommy, Ace, his ex-wives, etc., but he backs it up with interesting, never-before-heard (for the most part) stories. As a longtime fan, I often found myself reading passages and thinking, \\"I can't believe he put that in print.\\" The only people that really seem to be spared are Gigi (and you learn that that relationship hasn't always been smooth sailing either), Chris Lendt (I don't think I've ever read a bad word about Lendt from anyone), his daughter and a few close friends and family. And it was interesting to read how Peter's own parents got pissed at him about his marriage to Deb, how he treated them at times, etc.<br /><br />The one thing you'll appreciate about this book is its honesty. As a long time KISS fan, you want to feel bad for Peter, but you learn he was/is his own worst enemy. He takes the blame for most of his problems, and you don't see that often (and certainly not from any current or former KISS members). The bottom line is this: You will love this book. Period. You can tell Peter is a very, very passionate and emotional guy. Whereas Gene seems oblvious to his demons, and Ace is a conman to his demons, Peter is almost like a kid who screws up and says, \\"I don't know what I was thinking.\\" And you get the impression he really doesn't, and he battles his demons day in and day out trying to find happiness.<br /><br />So if you're expecting a happy-go-lucky, everything-was-great romp through the KISS years, buckle-up...this ain't it. This is a sometimes depressing, raw account of what the Catman went through as the drummer of The Hottest Band In The World. And even though it seems to have a happy ending, you can't help but think that this story isn't over yet...And what makes it such a great read is you feel the danger that is Peter Criss' life. You're just hoping his final bow will be a happy one.

0393088774|5.0|Krugman makes it clear in the kind of prose that even middle school students can appreciate that what we need now is more spending not less. The problem for most of us is that we think about the US government's finances in the way we think about our household or small business finances. If we spend more than we take in we are in trouble. However the US economy as a whole doesn't work that way and neither does the government. As Krugman observes in reference to why we are still in what he calls (variously) \\"a slump,\\" \\"a great recession,\\" and in the title, \\"a depression\\": \\"...your spending is my income and my income is your spending.\\" He asks, \\"if ordinary citizens are tightening their belts--spending less--and the government also spends less, who is going to buy American products?\\" (p. 28)<br /><br />So the solution to our economic problem, Krugman insists, is not austerity (which might work for households) but the opposite. We need the government to spend money to create jobs so that people can buy other people's goods and services. We especially need some infrastructure building here at home instead of in the Middle East.<br /><br />\\"Collectively,\\" Krugman asserts, \\"the world's residents are trying to buy less stuff than they are capable of producing, to spend less than they earn. That's possible for an individual, but not for the world as a whole. And the result is the devastation all around us.\\" (p. 30)<br /><br />The other thing to understand about governments, especially huge governments like the US with a $15-trillion a year economy is that government intervention can smooth out a crisis. This is because the US will not run out of people to buy its debt since its tax base is so huge that the risk of default is miniscule. When the economy gets back on its feet tax revenues will increase and the debts will be paid. Well, not paid in full. That is unlikely to ever happen, since it makes little sense. To borrow to buy something you don't need like luxuries is not wise. (Wars are usually luxuries for governments.) But to borrow to help grow the economy is a fine investment. Sound companies borrow because borrowing allows them to take advantage of their knowhow in producing goods and services that people will buy allowing the company to make money. Borrowing to party big time to impress the neighbors or your girlfriend grows no wealth. (Wars are sometimes shock and awe parties for heads of state looking to stay in power.)<br /><br />Aside from offering the solution to our economic woes in simple, straightforward terms, Krugman also does an outstanding job of explaining how we got into this mess in the first place. I've read several books and a number of articles explaining the mortgage crisis, the \\"too big to fail\\" bank welfare fraud and the derivatives hustles, but nowhere is this spelled out in as clear as fashion as Krugman does here. He is simply the best economist writing for an informed non-professional public at work today. This is not to mention that he is also a Nobel Prize winning economist.<br /><br />As for wages being too high, Krugman writes:<br /><br />\\"...today it's often argued that more labor market `flexibility'--a euphemism for wage cuts--is what we really need\\" (to cure high unemployment). \\"But while an individual worker can improve his chances of getting a job by accepting a lower wage, because that makes him more attractive compared to other workers, an across-the-board cut in wages leaves everyone in the same place, except for one thing: it reduces everyone's income, but the level of debt remains the same. So more flexibility in wages (and prices) would just make matters worse.\\" (pp. 52-53)<br /><br />I think the average person, even the fairly well educated average person, doesn't really understand how banks work and how they make money. I didn't until I was well into my fifties. Certainly the core of the Tea Party doesn't, although some of the supporters of financial institution deregulation do and that is precisely why they want deregulation. Here's how Krugman explains this in part:<br /><br />First he notes that the Glass-Steagall act of 1933 primarily did two things. It \\"established the Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation (FDIC) which guaranteed (and still guarantees) depositors against loss if their bank should happened to fail\\" (p. 59) Additionally, \\"Glass-Steagall limited the amount of risk banks could take. This was especially necessary given the establishment of deposit insurance, which could have created enormous `moral hazard.' That is, it could have created a situation in which bankers could raise lots of money, no questions asked--hey, it's all government-insured--then put that money into high-risk, high stakes investments, figuring that it was heads they win, tails the taxpayers lose.\\"<br /><br />Krugman then reminds us that this is exactly what happened during the savings and loan scandal of the Reagan administration. Likewise, the big investments banks knew during the later years of the George W. Bush administration that they were in fact too big to fail and the government in order to prevent a massive financial meltdown would have to bail them out if their Pandora's Box of risky derivatives (and other \\"financial instruments\\") went toxic. This knowledge gave them free rein to gamble like drunken sailors--well, that knowledge and the (how sweet it is!) deregulation of investment banking that took place primarily in the Reagan, Clinton and George W. Bush administrations. Toxic those gambles went and both the Bush and the Obama administrations found themselves with no choice but to bail the banks out lest the whole economy come tumbling down.<br /><br />One of the results of deregulation has been the enormous increase in the wealth of the top one percent (yes, those people) and what has happened to the real income of most of the rest of us. Krugman has two charts on page 74 showing the growth in household income from 1947 to the present. While the rich have indeed gotten richer the average family has seen its income growth \\"slowed to a crawl.\\"<br /><br />But it's even worse than Krugman makes it appear. That's because the only reason middle income Americans have been able to tread water is because many of those families became two income families. In other words the head of household's real income has actually fallen.<br /><br />Another factor in the actual decline in the average worker's buying power and the amazing increase in CEO compensation comes about, Krugman suggests, because worker's unions have lost a lot of their power. \\"It's surely relevant here to note the sharp decline in unionization during the 1980s, which removed one major player that might have protested huge paychecks for executives.\\" (p. 82)<br /><br />One more point. Krugman argues that the harsh austerity measures currently being acted out in Greece and other places in Europe are not only mistaken but based on a kind of \\"morality play\\" mentality. We all understand how it feels when our neighbors get away with something like buying houses they can't afford. We don't want the government to bail them out. They were fiscally irresponsible and should have to pay the piper. However even if that is true it doesn't help us by administering punishment in the form taking place in Greece, Ireland, Spain, and elsewhere. Our standard of living will suffer if we place our desire to punish others ahead of our doing what is necessary to grow the economy. It would help a lot if somehow some of the mortgage indebtedness were to be forgiven, is what Krugman suggests.<br /><br />In short, there's a tremendous amount of economic wisdom in this book, so much so I would recommend it as a supplement to a college macroeconomics text. You'll find that a number of the sometimes difficult ideas in those texts are illuminated almost incidentally by Krugman as he explains how we got into this mess and how we can get out. I wish this were required reading for high school students and the members of the Congress of the United States.<br /><br />--Dennis Littrell, author of \\"The World Is Not as We Think It Is\\"rich have indeed gotten richer the average family has seen its income growth \\"slowed to a crawl.\\"<br /><br />But it's even worse than Krugman makes it appear. I wish this were required reading for high school students and the members of the Congress of the United States.<br /><br />--Dennis Littrell, author of \\"The World Is Not as We Think It Is\\"

0393088774|5.0|Whether one agrees with Krugman's neo-Keynesian approach or not (and I generally do although I know other camps such as the Austrians don't, and I too find some value in the Austrian ideas) Krugman is the most approachable writer on economics out there today for the non-specialist. He writes clearly and concisely and he has a way of making complex issues easy to understand.<br /><br />In this book Krugman gives his ideas on what caused the Great Recession and how to end it. One of the best parts of the book is on pages 62-64 where he debunks the claim that government \\"forced\\" banks to give loans to people who couldn't afford to pay them back. For one thing, the loans for commercial real estate and to real estate developers and speculators were greater than the loans to private individuals. And there were more loans to people living in the wealthier suburbs and exurbs than the poorer inner city residents. In fact, most of the loans being issued weren't even covered by the Community Reinvestment Act of the Clinton Administration. The history of science is filled with beautiful theories killed by nasty little facts and this is yet another one.<br /><br />But the book is far more than just that. The most important part of Krugman's discussion has to do with liquidity traps and how to deal with them. This is partly what his Nobel Prize was for, as well as international trade and the new economic geography. Krugman explains how the economy fell into a recession where people were willing to produce more than they spent, businesses were paying down debt rather than spending (resulting in the odd but self-defeating problem where their assets were declining even as they tried to pay them down, causing them to lose even more money) resulting in a shortage of demand. Krugman's response is for the government to offer bonds that people could then buy. This would give the excess savings that people were hoarding a useful place to go and that would act to stimulate the economy.<br /><br />Whether one agrees with him or not, the other schools appear to offer approaches based on older ideas that just don't seem to have worked in the past. At least this one is new and and who knows, maybe it would work.<br /><br />Of course, there are camps that argue that government stimulus programs are a bad idea and never work. Obviously, this is false if one looks at what happened to the countries affected by the Great Depression in the 1930s. Most countries didn't try a stimulus package approach because back then we didn't know what we do now. The U.S. tried a half-hearted one under Roosevelt which really didn't do much. (Although if one checks the economic figures the U.S. did have a modest recovery in 1937 as a result of projects like the Tennessee Valley Authority and so on). However, the Japanese implemented a much bigger one, and amazingly within a year the economy had not only recovered but was starting to overheat so much that the government had to discontinue the stimulus. But it worked.<br /><br />Of course, camps like the Austrians and others who don't like even the faintest hint of any government action to moderate the worst extremes of the business cycle will probably just counter the Japan example by saying the Japanese workers are just smarter, better educated, and harder working than American workers. What's a poor neo-Keynesian to do? LOL<br /><br />Finally, for those who don't like Krugman there's one very good reason to listen to him. In a study of the predictions of several dozen prominent economists Krugman was correct 13 out of 15 times. Most were no better than random chance or slightly better, and some were worse. Krugman's record was statistically significant at the .001 level of confidence for those of you who have some background in stats. But basically, that figure means that there was less than a one in a thousand chance of that result being because of sampling error or bias in the sample.<br /><br />Overall, this is a clear andwell written book on our current problem. Whether one agrees with Krugman's neo-Keynesian approach he's always worth reading for his ideas and his clear and cogent presentation of the relevant economic principles and facts.well written book on our current problem. Whether one agrees with Krugman's neo-Keynesian approach he's always worth reading for his ideas and his clear and cogent presentation of the relevant economic principles and facts.

Monroe also published  a number scholarly works on Early Music. One of Monroe's early passions was the study of early musical instruments which he collected until this his death, and are now part of his historical archives. Monroe began a project to make recordings of musical instruments from the Medieval and Renaissance Periods during 1973-74. Monroe was himself was a respected musician in own right on the instruments of the period, and played many  of the included examples himself.<br /><br />After producing these recordings, Monroe started to compile a history based on his previous extended research.<br />The results of that research were a 90 page illustrated  volume entitled \\"Instruments of the Middle Ages and Renaissance (1976).  Some of these initial volumes were issued with  the accompanying musical recordings.<br /><br />The current 2 CD set consists of: CD1: Instruments of the MIddle Ages with 30  audio examples of Instruments of  Medieval musical instruments with period music. CD2  has 31 audio examples of Instruments of the Renaissance  playing Renaissance music. All examples were recorded by members of The Early Music Consort of London under David Monroe. Many of the actual example were recorded by, or with David Monroe.<br /><br />Some might consider this CD more of an academic exercise of some 61 short musical selections. To a certain extent this is accurate summation which memorializes the historical legacy of David Monroe. This is a rather unique and specialized reference tool for the musical instruments from the Medieval and Renaissance Periods. 0375703764|3.0|To give this book mere 3 out of 5 stars suggests that House of Leaves is a mediocre work. It isn't. But 3/5 is still the closest approximation of my feelings after reading it, no matter how inadequate the rating system is in expressing the book's flawed genius.<br /><br />House of Leaves has been marketed as a horror story, and a lesser author than Mr Danielewski would no doubt have turned it into a very engaging and still original piece of horror fiction. But the author has aimed higher, far higher.<br /><br />Just saying that House of Leaves works on many levels would be a rather trite observation; that much is obvious after even the most cursory glance. At the book's heart is a false document of sorts, which has been edited by one hand, then one more layer has been added by the character who, I guess, can be said to be the main narrator - and then there's one more layer of editors. Sound complicated? It is. For those who 'get' the book, all those layers probably are one of the hooks that draw them in, each level adding new meanings to decipher and discuss. I, on the other hand, was first confused, then irritated, and finally increasingly bored. At one level, it has been suggested, the book is a parody of post-modern textual criticism. If House of Leaves indeed is such an inside joke writ large, then I'm one of the outsiders.<br /><br />House of Leaves has attracted a considerable cult following. The sort of following who, for example, debate the true meaning of something that in a more ordinary book would have been dismissed as a typo. I can very well believe that Mr Danielewski has crafted the book to perfection, that every word, letter and space is just as it should be. But I wasn't fascinated enough to begin the quest of unravelling all the meanings encoded therein.<br /><br />House of Leaves will remain in my bookshelf. I have this uncanny feeling that one day I might try again - and be sucked in, becoming one with cult. More probably not, but I like to keepmy options open.

159420411X|5.0|This book is similar to Steven Levitt's [[ASIN:0060731338 Freakonomics: A Rogue Economist Explores the Hidden Side of Everything (P.S.)]], Nassim Taleb's [[ASIN:081297381X The Black Swan: Second Edition: The Impact of the Highly Improbable: With a new section: \\"On Robustness and Fragility\\"]], and James Surowiecki's [[ASIN:0385721706 The Wisdom of Crowds]].  All four books explore the intersection of data, human behavior, and outcomes.  They explain how to quantify outcomes within the financial markets, professional sports or elections.<br /><br />This book is especially interesting because Nate Silver has honed firsthand his statistical skills onto numerous domains including professional poker, baseball performance forecasting (he developed one of the best software program to do that), political elections (his \\"fivethirtyeight\\" blog).  And, when he is not a firsthand practitioner he is a first class investigator.<br /><br />The first seven chapters cover the errors and successes people have had in forecasting in various disciplines.  Chapter eight is the most pedagogical, as the author explains the basics of Bayes Theorem that he considers as an overall solution to many of the errors we make in forecasting.  The last five chapters focus on Bayesian thinking within various disciplines.<br /><br />Nate Silver's coverage of the credit rating agencies \\"Catastrophic failure of prediction\\" (first chapter title) is excellent.  In a single sentence on page 13, he captures the cause of the financial crisis: \\"In advance of the financial crisis, the system was so highly leveraged that a single lax assumption in the credit rating agencies played a huge role in bringing down the whole global financial system.\\"  Silver states that the AAA rated CDOs were deemed to have a default rate of only 0.12%.  The actual default rate was 28% or over 200 times greater!  This was because the rating agencies missed out the correlation between mortgage default rates at different locations when a nationwide home price downturn hit (see figure 1.2 on page 28.  Watch out that he mislabeled column 3 and 4 from the right).  Silver assesses that overall leverage was too high during the housing bubble.  Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac had a debt-to-equity leverage of 70-to-1.  Lehman Brothers and other investment banks were leveraged over 30-to-1.  Borrowers had often loan-to-value ratios of 100% on their homes.  The volume of credit default swaps, MBS, CDOs represented 30 to 60 times the volume of home sales during the bubble years (fig. 1.5 page 35).  Nate Silver summarizes the errors made.  Investors trusted the rating agencies.  The rating agencies assumed home prices would never decline on a nationwide basis because they never had since the Great Depression.  Lenders and borrowers believed rising home prices would bail them out through refinancing.  Policymakers believed the financial system had enough capital and was self-disciplined.  And, economists completely missed the ensuing severe recession.<br /><br />Nate Silver focuses next on political predictions.  This field of experts was so bad at predicting it motivated him to enter it by starting his fivethirtyeight blog.  He documents their failings extensively.  Within this chapter he refers to the theory of Philip Tetlock, professor of psychology and political science at Berkeley.  Tetlock had surveyed predictions of experts in various fields.  And, he categorized them within two archetypes: the hedgehogs and the foxes.  The hedgehogs are dogmatic, rarely change their minds, and are very confident of their forecast.  The foxes are just the opposite.  They update their forecasts as often as new information warrants it.  As a result, they make better forecasts.<br /><br />The chapter on baseball is one of the best because of Silver's extensive firsthand experience.  He uncovers many concepts applicable to many sports such as  the age-curve of baseball performance (pg. 81).  All sports have a predetermined age-curve.  Actually, every single aspects of life including life itself have predetermined age-curves.  His description of what it takes to be a successful professional baseball player (pg. 97) has also surprisingly broad applications.  The conclusion of the chapter is also fascinating.  It describes baseball management as a competitive arms race of intelligence gathering to extract small competitive edges.  And, that those competitive edges are short-lived.  That's a very interesting application of the Efficient Market Hypothesis.<br /><br />The chapter on economists documents how inaccurate their forecasts are.  The majority can't forecast a recession that has already started as they missed out on the three most recent ones (1990, 2001, 2007).  In November 2007, the average economic forecast was 2.4% real GDP growth in 2008.  Instead, real GDP shrank by -3.3%.  Economists assigned only a 1-in-2000 chance of the economy shrinking that much.  Yet, home prices were already declining.  Foreclosures had picked up.  Bear Stearns had gone belly up six months ago.  Those were powerful signals the housing and financial markets were on the edge of a cliff.  Also, economists are way too confident.  The few times you can extract confidence intervals from the economic profession they are invariably way too narrow because they underestimate the error level within their forecasts (pg. 182).  Nate Silver states that: \\"this property of overconfident prediction has been observed also in medical research, political science, finance, and psychology\\" (pg. 183).  Despite our having so much more data and computer power at our hands, economic forecasting has not improved since 1968.  This is because our underlying understanding of cause and effects has not changed much since.<br /><br />Chapter 8 introduces Bayes's Theorem.  Here Nate Silver often refers to a very good book on the subject: [[ASIN:0300188226 The Theory That Would Not Die: How Bayes' Rule Cracked the Enigma Code, Hunted Down Russian Submarines, and Emerged Triumphant from Two Centuries of Controversy]] by Sharon Bertsch McGrayne.<br /><br />Chapter 9 and 10 about chess and poker are excellent.  Kasparov was ultimately beaten by a computer bug.  IBM Big Blue made a move late in the last game that did not make any sense (the team who programmed it confirmed it was due to a small programming bug).  Kasparov who was in a vulnerable position could not figure out that move and in despair resigned the game and lost the series.  The Pareto principle of prediction on page 312 and 314 and the ensuing economics of poker are really interesting.  Poker winning are heavily dependent on the one worst player at a table.  If he leaves, the winnings are a lot harder to reap.<br /><br />Chapter 11 on the Efficient Market Hypothesis (EMH) is excellent.  Nate Silver states that the stock market is efficient most of the time, although it is never perfectly efficient (that would preclude a market).  But, it can be wildly inefficient on few occasions associated with bubbles and crashes.  Nate Silver demonstrates how both technical analysis and fundamental analysis do not beat the market over the long run.  Fig 11.3 on page 340 shows no correlation between the performance of mutual funds over the 2002 to 2006 period vs over the 2007 to 2011 period.  Past performance is no guarantee of future returns.  Next, Silver refers to Robert Shiller in showing the market is not as efficient as the EMH entails.  Shiller looked at the P/E ratio of the S&P 500 over a trailing 10 year period and looked at prospective returns.  And, the longer the period contemplated the greater the negative correlation between trailing P/E levels and future average yearly returns.  This suggests that the market can get overvalued.  But, the return correction is not apparent until looking at average return over a 10 to 20 year period.  Next, Nate Silver refers to the works of Richard Thaler and Daniel Kahneman in behavioral economics to outline how market traders are not perfectly rational.  They suffer from herd mentality, overconfidence, and being overly emotional rendering their trading pro-cyclical.<br /><br />So, if the market is not so efficient, can you beat it?  Probably not.  On page 345, Nate Silver demonstrates how a hypothetical investor with perfect timing over a decade (1976-1986) would get killed by very small transaction costs.  Even though this investor would handily beat the stock market before transaction costs, he would wipe out most of his capital after transaction costs.  Silver next tests a prudent investment strategy over the 1970 to 2009 period.  He assumes an investor is prudent and sells his position in the S&P 500 index whenever it had declined 25% from its peak and reinvests whenever it recovered 90% of its value.  Such an investor would have earned only 2.6% per year vs close to 10% for a simple buy-and-hold strategy.  Nate Silver does believe several hedge funds can beat the market.  But, they have intellectual and technological resources that no retail investor and few mutual funds can match.<br /><br />Chapter 12 on climate change is really interesting.  He differentiates between where scientists agree and disagree.  They all agree that the greenhouse effect exists and keeps the Earth warmer than it would otherwise be; that temperatures have risen over the past century; that greenhouse gases have contributed to that trend; and that water vapor is by far the most potent greenhouse gas (not CO2 as the Media conveys).  The majority of scientists agree that rising CO2 concentration does contribute to rising temperature.  But, there is a debate regarding how much.  Where the scientific community is more divergent is regarding climate models and projections.  They acknowledge that Al Gore's [[ASIN:B000ICL3KG An Inconvenient Truth]] deterministic apocalyptic message was way off base.<br /><br />Nate Silver explains why there is much uncertainty regarding climate models' projections.  One uncertainty is figuring out CO2 levels 100 years down the road.  Another uncertainty is getting the causal relationships right (there is a lot more than CO2 at play).  Another uncertainty concerns whether those models are programmed correctly.  Within the vast quantities of computer codes, are there a few bugs that contribute to generating erroneous forecasts?  Nate Silver reviews the prediction of the IPCC's 1990 model and observes that temperatures have not risen as fast as the model predicted.  Current temperatures are below the model's 95% confidence interval.  This lead the IPCC to reduce their baseline temperature increase from 3 degree Celsius per century in 1990 to 1.8 degree in 1995.  On page 407, Silver comes up with an interesting application of Bayes theorem applied to rising temperature predictions.<br /><br />The last chapter on terrorism is intriguing.  Terrorist attacks follow a similar Power Law as earthquakes.  The frequency of events declines exponentially with increase in intensity.  More violent events are much rarer than lesser ones.  But, the few major events dominate the data in human casualties.  For instance, 9/11 represented more than half of the total fatalities from terror attacks in NATO countries since 1979.  Thus, it is worth exploring means of mitigating the impact of such events.Russian Submarines, and Emerged Triumphant from Two Centuries of Controversy]] by Sharon Bertsch McGrayne.<br /><br />Chapter 9 and 10 about chess and poker are excellent. 1400032717|3.0|I finally got to The Curious Incident of the Dog in the Nighttime by Mark Haddon last week, and am so very glad I did. A couple months ago my wife tried to read it and put it down after maybe twenty pages, frustrated at the curious (and at times confusing) flow of the story, which is written from the perspective of an autistic 15-year-old boy. Part of the uniqueness (or the confusion, depending on your way of thinking) which isn't explained until a few chapters in is that the chapters are marked with ascending Prime numbers, not linear numbers. So, `Chapter 1' of the book is actually 2., the first prime number, then 3., 5., 7., 11. and so on.The reason for this is that Christopher, the protagonist, adores prime numbers and can recite all of them up to 7,057. He also loves the color red, but despises yellow and brown things, and refuses to let others touch him, including his parents. These are just a few of his many quirks that are revealed as the story goes on.<br /><br />Christopher is also revealed to be incredibly clever and makes up with genius whatever his social skills lack (which is a lot). The book opens with Christopher going out on one of his regular late night walks around the neighborhood and discovering a small poodle, dead, skewered with a garden fork. This sets his obsessive personality on an unquenchable quest to know why this dog is dead and who has done the deed. In that regard, it is mildly a `mystery' story, but this event is really only a springboard for the author to really unfold Christopher's character.<br /><br />Since it is Christopher actually `writing' the book, the chapters leap around a lot and describe what is going on in Christopher's head. His interactions with people are mostly very flat and calculated, and he only likes a very few of the people in his life because the rest of them just confuse him. The pages are filled with little pictures, mathematical proofs and drawings in between the text which Christopher has helpfully included to help flesh out his daily experiences. These are a fun addition that give a more personal impression that you are perhaps reading someone's journal or diary rather than a novel.<br /><br />Haddon writes with unique voice and well communicates how an autistic boy might think and evaluate the world. He also does a fantastic job of weaving in reverent and sometimes sad observations on the human condition through the mouth of this strange yet very likeable young man. Christopher's black and white way of seeing things is often humorous (to the reader, not to him) and pokes fun at the things we do so regularly that are maybe a bit ridiculous once you think about it.<br /><br />Ever since I read Jonathan Safran Foer's Extremely Loud & Incredibly Close (which to this day I can pretty safely say is my favorite book ever), I've been subconsciously on the hunt for another book with so much character and creativity. Curious Incident is largely different and I didn't love it quite as much as I do Foer's book, but it's probably the closest second I've ever found; oddly enough both books are written from the perspectives of young boys with lots of imagination. I also appreciate it when an author goes outside the norm with typesetting, which this book does to some degree.<br /><br />Overall, it was a fun and engaging read, with a surprising amount of depth and emotion. Also, a pretty impressive first novel.d to help flesh out his daily experiences. These are a fun addition that give a more personal impression that you are perhaps reading someone's journal or diary rather than a novel.<br /><br />Haddon writes with unique voice and well communicates how an autistic boy might think and evaluate the world. 0345404475|3.0|Blade Runner came out in 1982, but I was only introduced to it about 5 years ago when a film-buff sort of friend insisted we sit down and watch it together. He showed me The Director's Cut, which is one of several alternate edits of the film. Watching it with this friend, named Stewart, vastly enriched my first experience with the movie because he was able to talk me through some of the underlying themes and point out key moments that nerds have debated over for decades. By the end of my first time seeing it through, I wasn't entirely sure what I had just seen or what it meant, but I was highly intrigued and almost immediately wanted to watch it again. Since that time I've watched it at least once a year, and have come to prefer The Final Cut, the most recently released and most extensive version of the film.<br /><br />Last week, I read the 1968 novel Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep? which Blade Runner is loosely based on, in fact I can't stress enough just how loosely. It's more like the movie takes a couple of key ideas from Androids and creates it's own totally separate story that might take place in the same universe as the book. Understand, I'm not bashing the movie at all, in fact I think it is quite a remarkable film that accomplishes much on it's own, largely due to Ridley Scott's impressive directorial vision. It's a movie I can watch over and over and not grow tired of. This is because it's just a visually fascinating sci-fi cult-classic, but also because there are so many mysterious and ambiguous moments that no matter how many times and how closely you watch them, you're not quite sure what they're supposed to mean...<br /><br />I picked up the book trying my best to keep the movie out of my mind and just approach it as a completely separate entity. This worked pretty well. The book's first half focuses heavily on Rick Deckard's strong desire to own an authentic living animal. In Philip K. Dick's version of the future, real animals are extremely expensive and have become largely extinct. Humans instead own electronic mimics of animals if they cannot afford the real thing, but this is a shameful societal position to be in and one would never want to have to admit their animal was fake.<br /><br />Along with animals, there are virtually flawless humanoid robots, called Replicants, living (illegally) among humans on Earth that have immigrated secretly from off-world colonies. The most advanced and human-like of these androids are models called Nexus-6. Most of them are infused with false memories and not even aware that they are robots. Rick Deckard's job is to seek out these androids, confirm that they are not human by way of an eye-dilation test that measures empathy, and `retire' them if they are in fact replicants. He works for a sort of branch of police and is simply titled a bounty hunter. The movie obviously calls him a blade runner, but that term is nowhere to be found in the book.<br /><br />Though it had some unique ideas, I would be hard-pressed to necessarily call Androids a good book... I will say it baffles me that this book was written in the sixties, and I do firmly believe that Dick had an imagination far beyond his time. Yet his writing leaves a little something to be desired in the sense that his characters' dialogue is at times choppy and I found it difficult to discern exactly what he was trying to portray through them. It's hard to explain, but I felt that at times his flow would skip a beat and leave me, the reader, a bit in the dark as to how he had gotten to that current moment. I'm completely willing to admit that this may have been intentional on his part and was used as a literary device, and that I'm just simply not getting it. There are definitely some tones in the book about the blurring of dreams and reality, which makes for some abstract, dream-like scenes. Another strong theme is what truly defines being human.<br /><br />I find myself having the rare opinion that in this case, the movie was ultimately better than the book. But really that's not exactly fair because they have so little to do with each other that they ought to be considered separately. I can say that reading the book did enrich the movie for me in small ways, and helped me further understand the roots of some of the movie's themes.<br /><br />So, if you've read the book: good for you. If you haven't read it but enjoy the movie: don't even worry about it. If you haven't seen the movie: you're a silly goose. Go rent it immediately, experience it for yourself, then deconstruct it endlessly with the rest of us.he movie was ultimately better than the book. 0451169514|5.0|This weekend, I finished Stephen King's 1985 monster-novel IT (monster in size that is, although I suppose monster in subject to some degree too). I believe I started it in July, and just now finished it... Granted, I've been reading a few other things, and it is 1092 pages long in paperback.<br /><br />The book was written before I was born, so obviously it's been around awhile and is no mystery to the literary world. The TV-miniseries in the 90's obviously added to it's popularity and has made it one of King's best-known (and loved) works. All that to say, this review is about 25 years late, and probably everything said in it has been said before, but hey, just think of me as a second-generation King reader who is re-discovering his talent.<br /><br />Anyone who knows even the smallest bit about this book probably knows it is (in the most basic sense) about a clown terrorizing some kids, and then coming back and haunting them 27 years later as adults. Sounds simple right? Then why the 1000+ pages? Because King is a master of elaboration and detail, although in my opinion not the `boring kind' (like in English fiction when some cheeky fellow takes 50 pages to tell you what the grass on the rollicking hills is like).<br /><br />If I may make the comparison, in some of his books, King is similar to Quentin Tarantino's storytelling. Let me explain. King's character development often involves letting you just `hang out' with the characters for a while; moving through life with them and experiencing some moments that do give great depth to the person, but don't necessarily move the plot forward directly. If you think about, Tarantino often does this same thing. His movies are usually long. I think my point can be most clearly seen in Death Proof, which is not necessarily a favorite of mine, but the full version is nearly three hours, and practically half of that time is spent with the characters, just sitting around talking. Their dialogue isn't really relevant to the tale, but it expands your perception and understanding of them. Without beating the comparison to death, I think King uses a similar method.<br /><br />In the book, a significant amount of pages are spent with each of the 6 main characters (there is also a 7th on whom significantly less time is spent) as children and as adults; the narrative sort of switches back and forth in time from 1958 to 1985. There are also 5 first-person Interludes about the city of Derry itself, in which the book takes place.<br /><br />With such a monstrous novel, it is hard to know where to begin or what to focus on, so I'll guess I'll just mention a few things. The characters are great; very well balanced, yet diverse in personality, which makes for great dialogue, and each one's talents or abilities end up playing a key role in defeating IT. King also does an amazing job of portraying the world from an 11-year-old's viewpoint, which is about how old they are in the beginning of the book.<br /><br />The villains are superbly crazy and menacing. Apart from Pennywise the Clown himself and all the derivative shapes he takes on, there is a psychotic and intensely abusive husband who is married to the female main character in the `Grown-ups' part of the book, and a group of bullies with an equally psychotic leader in the `Kids' portion. This makes for many intense moments, and there are portions of the book where it seems nearly impossible for the protagonists to escape the mounting opposition against them (which is what good conflict is all about).<br /><br />The book is creepy. Really creepy. Pennywise is practically invincible, has lived for (at least suggestively) thousands of years, feeds on freaking little kids, and takes on the shape of whatever the seer is most afraid of. Within the first twenty pages, he rips off a little boy's arm trying to pull him down a storm drain. Did I mention the book is violent?<br /><br />Anyway, this is getting long and not going much of anywhere. But the book is fantastic; honestly one of the best books I've ever read. If you're okay with just hanging out with characters for awhile, intense gory violence, some sexual content, and a whole heck of a lot of detail, you'll probably enjoy this.<br /><br />I think the most incredible thing about the book is the scope of the world King creates. When you finish this book, take a breath, and think back over all the moments and events, you feel as if you have spent a lifetime with these characters, and had your nose right to the grind along with them through thick and thin. That, to me, is powerful fiction, and damn good storytelling.<br /><br />In other news, I just picked up King's new one that came out last week, Full Dark, No Stars. It's his 4th or 5th collection of Novellas (not even counting his half a dozen other short-story collections), or `short-novels,' most of them about 100 pages each or so. I very much appreciate that King still consistently publishes short works; one, because I think short-fiction is the hardest to do well, and second, because hardly anyone else is doing it anymore. I suppose it's not marketable enough unless you have a big name.<br /><br />After this one, I'll probably give King a break for a while, not because I'm sick of him, but because I have an ever-growing stack of modern-classics and other things that I'd like to get to. May I recommend reading IT over your next summer break? It'll take you the whole time, I guarantee.ook is fantastic; honestly one of the best books I've ever read. If you're okay with just hanging out with characters for awhile, intense gory violence, some sexual content, and a whole heck of a lot of detail, you'll probably enjoy this.<br /><br />I think the most incredible thing about the book is the scope of the world King creates. When you finish this book, take a breath, and think back over all the moments and events, you feel as if you have spent a lifetime with these characters, and had your nose right to the grind along with them through thick and thin. That, to me, is powerful fiction, and damn good storytelling.<br /><br />In other news, I just picked up King's new one that came out last week, Full Dark, No Stars. It's his 4th or 5th collection of Novellas (not even counting his half a dozen other short-story collections), or `short-novels,' most of them about 100 pages each or so. I very much appreciate that King still consistently publishes short works; one, because I think short-fiction is the hardest to do well, and second, because hardly anyone else is doing it anymore. I suppose it's not marketable enough unless you have a big name.<br /><br />After this one, I'll probably give King a break for a while, not because I'm sick of him, but because I have an ever-growing stack of modern-classics and other things that I'd like to get to. May I recommend reading IT over your next summer break? It'll take you the whole time, I guarantee.

While many find the reimagining of Peter's world a bit hard to take, this was one of the first movies I saw that did this. Where they take an old story and update it, but leaving the characters who they are supposed to be. Unlike the remake of Romeo and Juliet (starring Dicaprio) this reimagining makes since in the neverland world. This movie proves you are never too old to feel young. The truth of our existence lies in the lives that we touch. For better or for worse and despite our shortcomings there are those in this world who will always love us and need us. Growing up doesn't mean you have to get old. And this movie is timeless. Watch it again, and watch it with friends or with family or a child in your life. Watching their eyes light up will make you feel young again. Or watch this movie for the first time, and see how real storytelling can still be the most important part of a movie. CGI is great in some instances, but there is no substitution for real sets and real actors. So sit back and enjoy. Afterwards you'll feel as if you could chase down pirates too.

0374214913|3.0|Your appreciation for Penumbra is greatly dependent on your expectations. If you're interested in a snappy, YA page-turner to knock back like a light beer then Penumbra fits in nicely. If instead you're expecting intrigue and the light of exploration, then it is neither mysterious nor remarkable. Welterweight spoilers below.<br /><br />The story is set primarily in modern fantasy San Francisco where stereotypes about the Bay Area roam freely. As a recent transplant to SF I smirked at fairly accurate renditions of the corner of Broadway and Grant--the narrated location of the eponymous 24-Hour Bookstore--and little details like the way SF's winding streets reveal the bay at every opportunity. Unfortunately, the setting erodes rapidly into a technofantasy wonderland with the characters sudden fascination with computerized 3D modeling and the subsequent entry and romantic advances of Googler and Data Viz enthusiast Kat Potente. From this point forward (the author's?) unbridled infatuation with technology sets the stage and we're exposed to a nerdy successful entrepreneur stereotype named Neel and a very light \\"cryptomystery\\" involving a shadowy, but ultimately somewhat friendly, cult.<br /><br />In the unraveling of the cults secrets we witness details that seem born of a light obsession with typography, \\"hacking\\", books, cryptography, and perhaps trans-humanism. The disaffection of the narrator-protagonist speeds along the second half of the book with little trouble. The might of Google is tested and a literarily clever homage to, I imagine, Tolkein-named-Craig-Moffat succeeds where the techno-divinity failed. If you think the moral here is already too obvious you may want to skip out the (literal) slide deck toward the end.<br /><br />The book is densely peppered with references to modern antiquities. This novel's message might be generalized, but its medium is sharply 2012 and risks feeling passe by March 2013, trendy nostalgic by 2014, and utterly ancient by 2015.I don't think I've ever read any other work of fiction that so brazenly lives in the present. That said, all the detail backfires by making the technology and cryptography, themselves foundational elements of the narrative arc, detailed, yet terribly unbelievable.<br /><br />But I'm being too harsh. Sloan's writing has a wonderful lightness to it and is filled with infectious optimism. I almost want to say that my criticisms derive from my failure to just relax, MST3000-style. I can't recommend it to anyone who wants to learn or be challenged, but we can read just for enjoyment, too. Which is it's own kind of thesis for the book.<br /><br />Tl;dr? Optimism like Sloan's is definitely fun, but without a little more depth it's sadly, forgettable.

I liked the message (just live your life, enjoy the time you have, don't be a clock-watcher) but this short book felt like it was jammed full of padding.<br /><br />The book is about Dor, a man who lives at the beginning of civilization. He is happily married to his childhood sweetheart, has kids and is also the first man to measure time. He creates sundials, water clocks, measures the cycles of the moon and notices the days get longer and shorter as the year progresses. His childhood friend is the king and the creator of the Tower of Babel. His friend wants to harness this ability to measure time in some way to build his tower but Dor refuses. They are banished and eventually his wife dies from a disease. Dor returns to the tower and storms up the stairs just as God punishes the builders of the tower (sort of like it is described in Genesis). Dor is not killed. Instead he is placed in a cave and is forced to listen to the prayers and laments of everyone who wants more time, less time, to stop time, to reverse time, or whatever. Dor does not age even though time continues to move forward. He becomes the mythical figure  known as Father Time.<br /><br />Meanwhile, in the modern world, two people have their own issues with time. An elderly businessman who is dying from cancer wants more time. A high school girl is dealing with issues of bullying and divorced parents needs to slow things down so she can process them.<br /><br />Dor is finally released from his prison and told that he can finally die if he can help these two people from a future world that he can barely comprehend. He gets to use the tools of Father Time (controlling the speed of time) to help him accomplish this goal. Will he do it? Albom telegraphs that early on - of course. How will he do it? That's the question.<br /><br />Dan Stevens reads this book with a great deal of skill. His multiple female characters are well done. 0525952926|5.0|There is a danger of social and political historical fiction like the Century Trilogy. People already have a connection to the events and a sloppy author can simply trade on that knowledge to hold a reader's interest without allowing the reader to uncover something new.Historical fiction is often centered outside the experiences of the reader. When [[ASIN:1451635621 Gone with the Wind, 75th Anniversary Edition]] hit the best sellers list in 1936, there were both living veterans of the Civil War and many children of those veterans. GWTW reinforced much of the moonlight and magnolias myth of the Ante-Bellum South to such an extent that those myths still resonate one hundred fifty years after the War.<br /><br />Fortunately Ken Follett is an uncommon author. Covering the period from the end of the War to End All Wars to the end of the second such war, most readers are close enough to those events. However, Follett lets readers uncover new perspectives on these events.<br /><br />Many have written about the rise of Fascism in Europe and but few demonstrated how close the British aristocracy came to embracing it themselves. It is one thing to write about the Holocaust on a grand scale and something else altogether to witness the forced evacuation of the ill and insane from Jewish hospitals on trucks bound for extermination camps. The stupidity of American isolationism, the cruelty of Stalinism, idealism of the expatriate fighters in Spain, gay soldiers,and the bravery of the French and German resistance are all subjects within the reach of Follett's pen.<br /><br />Sit back, settle in and enjoy this part of what will be considered, for a long time to come, a contemporary gem.

B001TMCF3S|1.0|This review is for the Kindle edition of \\"The Complete Novels, Jane Austen (Author), Karen Joy Fowler (Introduction). Penguin Classics Deluxe Edition. ASIN: B001TMCF3S\\".<br /><br />I have a paper copy of this book (or rather another Penguin edition of it ISBN: 978-0141030173), but it's a bit bulky, so I bought the Kindle version of this book today. Only in a few minutes of browsing I found a couple of typos - a full-stop instead of space between words in the same sentence, a semi-colon where there should be a comma. Therefore I have returned it.<br /><br />I have also notified Amazon about these (using the `Report Content Error' menu on Kindle); so perhaps these particular ones will be quickly fixed. But, I am sure, there will be others.<br /><br />I verified all these errors against the paper copy, especially the 'semi-colon in place of comma' error because it is somewhat hard to be sure about this specific one in a Jane Austen text as in some places she does make use of a good number of semi-colons to construct long sentences; and I think it is a literary artifact she consciously employs to convey the meaning in a particular way or set a certain pace for a stream of thought, etc. Hence it is very important that her text be of high quality.<br /><br />Here's the semi-colon error. Note the semi-colon after 'fancy'. This is somewhere towards the end of Chapter 9 of Pride & Prejudice.<br /><br />  \\"And so ended his affection,\\" said Elizabeth impatiently. \\"There has been many a one, I fancy; overcome in the same way. I wonder who first discovered the efficacy of poetry in driving away love!\\"<br /><br />A previous reviewer (Cynthia L. Gause, October 24, 2012) has also reported spelling mistakes. She is right about there being 'riot' in the text, where it should be 'not' - (\\" ... but I am an idle fellow, and though I have riot many, I have more than I ever look into.\\" - Ch. 8, Pride & Prejudice). But as to the other examples she gives (\\"chuse\\" into \\"choose\\", \\"shew\\" into\\"show\\", \\"ancle\\" into \\"ankle\\", and \\"stile\\" into \\"style\\"), I think she thought them to be typos only because she expected a modernized-spelling edition. These are spellings that Jane Auten used and it seems a very common practice to retain them in modern editions; unlike most modern editions of Shakespeare, say, where spellings are modernized. For example, Oxford World's Classics, Penguin, David M. Shapard's annotated editions from Anchor, all use these spellings instead of their modern equivalents.<br /><br />I have a number of Penguin's Kindle books (as also their paper equivalents in most cases) but I had not noticed quality problems before. I had always thought of Penguin, along with others like Oxford, as publishers whose text could be implicitly trusted to be of very high quality; maybe I am naive. But in this case Penguin has clearly not lived up to that high expectation.<br /><br />I wanted this Kindle book very much, and I am sorry I had to return it. If Penguin releases a well-proofed version in the future I am sure I shall buy it again.<br /><br />But, in the meanwhile, I just wanted to warn prospective buyers, and hopefully alert Penguin (if, that is, anybody there reads these reviews) so that they can think about scheduling a revision for this book; hence this (somewhat long) review.

B008G8SFGO|2.0|It must be a tough job being a Mastering Engineer these days.  The job is to make a recording sound as good as possible while being pressured to have the recording get the most attention, in other words, BE THE LOUDEST.<br /><br />However, there is a limit on HOW LOUD music encoded to compact disc can be.  The only way to increase perceived loudness within this limitation is to apply compression, and this was done extensively on the CD version of this release.<br /><br />When I popped in the title, at about 6 seconds into Track 1, I noticed a harshness to the recording.  Drums sound best when they have a strong attack, with a natural decay.  Eric might as well be playing cardboard boxes with the drum tone on this album.  Such a big deal was made about how analog equipment was used during recording, but none of the natural warmth of such gear is present after the heavy-handed compression is applied to make the overall signal as loud as possible.<br /><br />Pop in any of your older Kiss CDs... perhaps the first to feature the drumming of Eric Singer, \\"Revenge\\", and do a comparison, especially of the drums... The tone is gone, the dynamics are gone. The vocals have a harshness during the louder vocal melodies.<br /><br />Hopefully all these artists who are destroying their own music have preserved the pre-compressed recordings so that once the loudness war has ended, they can release a full dynamic version of their albums.  The performances on this CD are great.  Kiss is back in form. Unfortunately they are following the trend of compressing the music to death, and Monster has fallen victim to the Loudness War.

B005LAII9Y|5.0|Going from a short, to a feature-length movie, my main question<br />walking in was, \\"What did Tim add?\\" The answer is \\"A lot\\", so much<br />in fact that I felt kind of disoriented with the story, compared<br />to what I remember of the original.<br /><br />It wasn't until the movie was over and I had some time to think<br />about it, that I began appreciate all this. I've seen a lot of<br />Tim Burton films, and I think he has a keen eye for what it means<br />to be human.<br /><br />With Frankenweenie, Tim has a lot to say, and he says it. The thing<br />is, he's communicating about things which other directors generally<br />don't. That means it takes some time and thought to pick up on what<br />he's saying. There's no cliches to get a handle on. Its kind of like<br />watching two movies at once, one is the apparent content, the other<br />is the meaning behind it.<br /><br />One of the best examples is the disjunction between the world of<br />kids, and the world of adults. In other movies this is basically<br />a social commentary, but in Frankenweenie the disjunction is much<br />more in the basic fabric of each world. When Tim first shows the<br />\\"shining faces\\" of the kids in Victor's science class, he shows them<br />as Victor sees them, not as Victor's parents would. Its a shock.<br />When Victor's father explains the art of his job, its a different<br />kind of shock, something we recognize as adults.<br /><br />Another example is what Tim is saying about the world as Sparky<br />sees it. This is where the movie really shines. Tim can add more<br />depth here in the remake, because Sparky is animated. Yet true to<br />the original, Tim allows Sparky to remain entirely a dog, and not<br />a human. The result is that Sparky is a very life-like dog character,<br />created with Tim's empathy and insights into Sparky and his world.<br />Some of the commentary here is profound. If you ever saw Family Dog,<br />you may see connections here.<br /><br />Going back to the added material, Tim decided that if he was going<br />to have a feature-length movie about a boy and his dog, he would<br />include a girl and her cat. Naturally these characters are, for the<br />most part, opposites of Victor and Sparky. Yet the cat is often like<br />a real cat, and the girl does have a human connection with her cat.<br />The characters are not caricatures, they're just, well, very different.<br />And in the end, sad.<br /><br />When Tim gets into the social relationships between the kids, he raises<br />different themes, makes broader comments about the world we live in.<br />I don't want to give it away, but he gets in deep on the topics of<br />being an individual, competing in a group, and the unforeseen consequences<br />that can arise from both. There is a lot of cause-and-effect going on<br />in this movie.<br /><br />In fact, there is a whole lot going on in this movie, period :-)

The units happily boot and run off the USB stick.  Each unit has an i7 processor and 4 gigs of ram.  Their CPU usage is 100% all the time as they fold protein models for the 'Folding at Home' project.  They have been running for 3 months continuously without overheating (thanks to the ICE cooling design) or needing rebooting (thanks to Ubuntu/Linux as the OS).<br /><br />I did get one unit that refused to POST (Power On Self Test).  After a little testing I found the the first memory slot was not responding.  Amazon sent a replacement unit immediately, and paid for the return shipping for the bad unit as well.  I could not have asked for better customer service; it took the sting out of getting a bad unit.<br /><br />If you plan to use this unit as a base for your personal computer, it is a fine choice.  I suggest going with an Intel processor that has the HD4000 in the CPU (Ivy Bridge); the performance from that is enough to handle most basic games.  I have five i7 units with HD3000 (Sandy Bridge) and one i7 unit with HD4000 (Ivy Bridge); the performance for basic games is much better with the HD4000 (I did some quick testing before they got sent to 'Server Farm Duty').<br /><br />There is room for up to a mid-level video card (these are limited more by power draw then physical card size) for gamers considering this unit.<br /><br />My only suggestion is to purchase your own thermal paste for the installation of the CPU as the paste included is rather stiff and hard to use (but it will work).
